In IDAs using optical detection, the host-guest interaction must be strong and selective enough to allow the indicator to be displaced by the analyte, resulting in a measurable optical signal (e.g., color change, fluorescence shift). Common types of host-guest interactions used in IDA design include:\n\n1. Hydrogen bonding: Hosts such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and crown ethers can form hydrogen bonds with guests, enabling selective recognition and displacement.\n2. π-π stacking: Aromatic hosts (e.g., cucurbiturils, calixarenes) can interact with aromatic indicators and analytes via π-π interactions.\n3. Electrostatic interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., sulfonated calixarenes, crown ethers) can bind oppositely charged guests, allowing for displacement upon analyte introduction.\n4. Hydrophobic interactions: Nonpolar hosts (e.g., cyclodextrins) can encapsulate hydrophobic indicators, which are displaced by more strongly binding hydrophobic analytes.\n5. Metal-ligand coordination: Hosts containing metal centers (e.g., transition metal complexes) can bind indicators via coordination bonds, which are displaced by analytes with higher affinity for the metal.\n\nThe choice of host-guest interaction depends on the chemical nature of the analyte and the desired selectivity and sensitivity. Optical detection is achieved by monitoring changes in the indicator's absorbance or fluorescence upon displacement, providing a convenient and sensitive readout for analyte detection.", In IDAs using optical detection, the host-guest interaction must be strong and selective enough to allow the indicator to be displaced by the analyte, resulting in a measurable optical signal (e.g., color change, fluorescence shift). Common types of host-guest interactions used in IDA design include:\n\n1. Hydrogen bonding: Hosts such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and crown ethers can form hydrogen bonds with guests, enabling selective recognition and displacement.\n2. π-π stacking: Aromatic hosts (e.g., cucurbiturils, calixarenes) can interact with aromatic indicators and analytes via π-π interactions.\n3. Electrostatic interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., sulfonated calixarenes, crown ethers) can bind oppositely charged guests, allowing for displacement upon analyte introduction.\n4. Hydrophobic interactions: Nonpolar hosts (e.g., cyclodextrins) can encapsulate hydrophobic indicators, which are displaced by more strongly binding hydrophobic analytes.\n5. Metal-ligand coordination: Hosts containing metal centers (e.g., transition metal complexes) can bind indicators via coordination bonds, which are displaced by analytes with higher affinity for the metal.\n\nThe choice of host-guest interaction depends on the chemical nature of the analyte and the desired selectivity and sensitivity. Optical detection is achieved by monitoring changes in the indicator's absorbance or fluorescence upon displacement, providing a convenient and sensitive readout for analyte detection.", In IDAs using optical detection, the host-guest interaction must be strong and selective enough to allow the indicator to be displaced by the analyte, resulting in a measurable optical signal (e.g., color change, fluorescence shift). Common types of host-guest interactions used in IDA design include:\n\n1. Hydrogen bonding: Hosts such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and crown ethers can form hydrogen bonds with guests, enabling selective recognition and displacement.\n2. π-π stacking: Aromatic hosts (e.g., cucurbiturils, calixarenes) can interact with aromatic indicators and analytes via π-π interactions.\n3. Electrostatic interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., sulfonated calixarenes, crown ethers) can bind oppositely charged guests, allowing for displacement upon analyte introduction.\n4. Hydrophobic interactions: Nonpolar hosts (e.g., cyclodextrins) can encapsulate hydrophobic indicators, which are displaced by more strongly binding hydrophobic analytes.\n5. Metal-ligand coordination: Hosts containing metal centers (e.g., transition metal complexes) can bind indicators via coordination bonds, which are displaced by analytes with higher affinity for the metal.\n\nThe choice of host-guest interaction depends on the chemical nature of the analyte and the desired selectivity and sensitivity. Optical detection is achieved by monitoring changes in the indicator's absorbance or fluorescence upon displacement, providing a convenient and sensitive readout for analyte detection.", In IDAs using optical detection, the host-guest interaction must be strong and selective enough to allow the indicator to be displaced by the analyte, resulting in a measurable optical signal (e.g., color change, fluorescence shift). Common types of host-guest interactions used in IDA design include:\n\n1. Hydrogen bonding: Hosts such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and crown ethers can form hydrogen bonds with guests, enabling selective recognition and displacement.\n2. π-π stacking: Aromatic hosts (e.g., cucurbiturils, calixarenes) can interact with aromatic indicators and analytes via π-π interactions.\n3. Electrostatic interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., sulfonated calixarenes, crown ethers) can bind oppositely charged guests, allowing for displacement upon analyte introduction.\n4. Hydrophobic interactions: Nonpolar hosts (e.g., cyclodextrins) can encapsulate hydrophobic indicators, which are displaced by more strongly binding hydrophobic analytes.\n5. Metal-ligand coordination: Hosts containing metal centers (e.g., transition metal complexes) can bind indicators via coordination bonds, which are displaced by analytes with higher affinity for the metal.\n\nThe choice of host-guest interaction depends on the chemical nature of the analyte and the desired selectivity and sensitivity. Optical detection is achieved by monitoring changes in the indicator's absorbance or fluorescence upon displacement, providing a convenient and sensitive readout for analyte detection.",
